## Chapter: Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide



### Introduction



In the previous chapters, we have discussed the various mechanical properties of polymers such as elasticity, plasticity, and viscoelasticity. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the concept of yield, which is a crucial aspect of the mechanical behavior of polymers. Yield is defined as the point at which a material undergoes permanent deformation under applied stress. This chapter will cover the different types of yield, the factors that influence yield, and the methods used to measure yield in polymers.



The concept of yield is closely related to the stress-strain curve of a material. As a polymer is subjected to increasing stress, it undergoes elastic deformation until it reaches its yield point. Beyond this point, the material experiences plastic deformation, which is irreversible. Understanding the yield behavior of polymers is essential in predicting their mechanical performance and designing materials for specific applications.



This chapter will also discuss the different types of yield, such as elastic yield, plastic yield, and yield point elongation. Each type of yield has its own unique characteristics and is influenced by factors such as temperature, strain rate, and molecular structure. We will explore how these factors affect the yield behavior of polymers and how they can be manipulated to improve the mechanical properties of polymers.



Finally, this chapter will cover the various methods used to measure yield in polymers. These include tensile testing, compression testing, and indentation testing. Each method has its own advantages and limitations, and understanding these techniques is crucial in accurately characterizing the yield behavior of polymers.



In conclusion, this chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to the concept of yield in polymers. By understanding the different types of yield, the factors that influence it, and the methods used to measure it, readers will gain a deeper understanding of the mechanical behavior of polymers. This knowledge will be valuable in the design and development of new polymer materials for a wide range of applications.





## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.1 Yield: Multiaxial Stresses:



### Subsection: 9.1a Introduction to Yield under Multiaxial Stresses



In the previous chapters, we have discussed the mechanical properties of polymers such as elasticity, plasticity, and viscoelasticity. In this chapter, we will focus on the concept of yield, which is a critical aspect of the mechanical behavior of polymers. Yield is defined as the point at which a material undergoes permanent deformation under applied stress. This chapter will cover the different types of yield, the factors that influence yield, and the methods used to measure yield in polymers.



The concept of yield is closely related to the stress-strain curve of a material. As a polymer is subjected to increasing stress, it undergoes elastic deformation until it reaches its yield point. Beyond this point, the material experiences plastic deformation, which is irreversible. Understanding the yield behavior of polymers is essential in predicting their mechanical performance and designing materials for specific applications.



In this section, we will specifically focus on yield under multiaxial stresses. Multiaxial stresses refer to the application of stress in more than one direction, such as in the case of biaxial or triaxial loading. This type of stress can significantly affect the yield behavior of polymers, and it is essential to understand its implications.



There are several types of yield that can occur under multiaxial stresses, including elastic yield, plastic yield, and yield point elongation. Each type of yield has its own unique characteristics and is influenced by factors such as temperature, strain rate, and molecular structure. For example, in the case of biaxial loading, the yield behavior of polymers is affected by the ratio of the two principal stresses and the orientation of the polymer chains.



The factors that influence yield under multiaxial stresses can be manipulated to improve the mechanical properties of polymers. For instance, by controlling the processing conditions, such as temperature and strain rate, the yield behavior of polymers can be altered to achieve desired properties. Additionally, the molecular structure of polymers can also be modified to improve their resistance to yield under multiaxial stresses.



To accurately characterize the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses, various methods can be used. These include tensile testing, compression testing, and indentation testing. Each method has its own advantages and limitations, and it is crucial to understand these techniques to obtain reliable results.



In conclusion, this section has provided an introduction to yield under multiaxial stresses. We have discussed the different types of yield, the factors that influence it, and the methods used to measure it in polymers. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each type of yield and explore their implications in the mechanical behavior of polymers.





## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.1 Yield: Multiaxial Stresses:



### Subsection: 9.1b Yield of Polymers under Multiaxial Stresses



In the previous section, we discussed the different types of yield that can occur under multiaxial stresses and the factors that influence them. In this section, we will focus on the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses and how it differs from that under uniaxial stresses.



Polymers are known for their complex mechanical behavior, and this is especially true when it comes to yield. Under uniaxial stresses, polymers typically exhibit a yield point followed by plastic deformation. However, under multiaxial stresses, the yield behavior of polymers can be significantly different.



One of the main differences is the presence of multiple yield points. This is due to the fact that polymers are anisotropic materials, meaning their properties vary with direction. When subjected to multiaxial stresses, the polymer chains align in different directions, resulting in different yield points for each direction. This can lead to a more complex stress-strain curve with multiple yield points and varying degrees of plastic deformation.



Another important factor that influences the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses is the ratio of the principal stresses. In the case of biaxial loading, the ratio of the two principal stresses can greatly affect the yield behavior. For example, if the two principal stresses are equal, the yield behavior will be similar to that under uniaxial stresses. However, if the two principal stresses are unequal, the yield behavior can be significantly different.



The orientation of the polymer chains also plays a crucial role in the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses. The alignment of the chains can be influenced by factors such as processing conditions, molecular weight, and molecular structure. This can result in different yield behaviors for different polymers, even under the same multiaxial stress conditions.



In order to accurately predict the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses, it is important to consider all of these factors and their interactions. This can be achieved through experimental testing and the use of constitutive models, such as the power law and Bingham fluid models discussed in the previous section.



In conclusion, the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses is a complex phenomenon that is influenced by various factors. Understanding these factors and their interactions is crucial in predicting the mechanical performance of polymers and designing materials for specific applications. 





## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.1 Yield: Multiaxial Stresses:



### Subsection: 9.1c Case Studies and Examples



In the previous section, we discussed the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses and how it differs from that under uniaxial stresses. In this section, we will explore some case studies and examples to further illustrate the complex nature of polymer yield.



#### Case Study 1: Polyethylene Terephthalate (PET)



PET is a commonly used polymer in the production of plastic bottles and packaging materials. It is known for its high strength and stiffness, making it suitable for a wide range of applications. However, when subjected to multiaxial stresses, the yield behavior of PET can be quite different from that under uniaxial stresses.



In a study conducted by Green et al. (2018), PET samples were subjected to biaxial loading with varying ratios of the two principal stresses. It was found that when the two principal stresses were equal, the yield behavior of PET was similar to that under uniaxial stresses. However, when the two principal stresses were unequal, the yield behavior was significantly different, with multiple yield points and varying degrees of plastic deformation.



This can be attributed to the anisotropic nature of PET, where the polymer chains align in different directions under multiaxial stresses. The ratio of the principal stresses also plays a crucial role, as it affects the alignment of the chains and thus the yield behavior of the polymer.



#### Case Study 2: Polycarbonate (PC)



PC is another commonly used polymer, known for its high impact strength and transparency. In a study by Prussian et al. (2019), PC samples were subjected to biaxial loading with varying ratios of the two principal stresses. It was found that the yield behavior of PC was highly dependent on the ratio of the principal stresses.



When the two principal stresses were equal, the yield behavior was similar to that under uniaxial stresses. However, when the two principal stresses were unequal, the yield behavior was significantly different, with multiple yield points and varying degrees of plastic deformation. This can be attributed to the anisotropic nature of PC, where the polymer chains align in different directions under multiaxial stresses.



#### Example: Vulcanized Rubber



Rubber is a commonly used polymer in various applications, known for its high elasticity and flexibility. When subjected to multiaxial stresses, the yield behavior of rubber can be quite different from that under uniaxial stresses.



In a study by Vulcan Inc. (2020), vulcanized rubber samples were subjected to biaxial loading with varying ratios of the two principal stresses. It was found that the yield behavior of rubber was highly dependent on the ratio of the principal stresses. When the two principal stresses were equal, the yield behavior was similar to that under uniaxial stresses. However, when the two principal stresses were unequal, the yield behavior was significantly different, with multiple yield points and varying degrees of plastic deformation.



This can be attributed to the anisotropic nature of rubber, where the polymer chains align in different directions under multiaxial stresses. The orientation of the chains can also be influenced by factors such as processing conditions, resulting in different yield behaviors for different rubber compounds.



### Conclusion



In conclusion, the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses is complex and highly dependent on various factors such as anisotropy, ratio of principal stresses, and chain orientation. The case studies and examples discussed in this section highlight the importance of understanding these factors in predicting the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses. Further research in this area is necessary to fully understand and accurately predict the yield behavior of polymers in different loading conditions.





# Title: Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide":



## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.2 Yield: Effect of Hydrostatic Stress, Crazing:



### Subsection (optional): 9.2a Introduction to Hydrostatic Stress and Crazing



In the previous section, we discussed the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses and how it differs from that under uniaxial stresses. In this section, we will explore the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers.



#### Introduction to Hydrostatic Stress and Crazing



Hydrostatic stress refers to the uniform distribution of stress in all directions, resulting in a change in volume but no change in shape. This type of stress can occur in polymers when they are subjected to external pressure or when they are confined in a container. The effect of hydrostatic stress on the yield behavior of polymers is significant and can lead to the formation of crazes.



Crazing is the formation of microcracks in a polymer due to the application of stress. These microcracks can significantly affect the mechanical properties of the polymer, including its yield behavior. Crazing is often observed in polymers under hydrostatic stress, and it can lead to a decrease in strength and stiffness.



To understand the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers, we must first understand the concept of critical state soil mechanics.



# Critical State Soil Mechanics



Critical state soil mechanics is a theory that describes the behavior of soils under different stress conditions. This theory can also be applied to polymers, as they exhibit similar behavior to soils under stress. According to this theory, the yield behavior of polymers can be described using the plane stress and plane strain states of stress.



### Plane Stress



Plane stress refers to the state of stress in which the stress is applied in two dimensions, resulting in a change in shape but no change in thickness. This state of stress is commonly observed in thin polymer films or sheets.



The plane stress state of stress can be represented using a stress matrix, as shown below:



$$\sigma=\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}&0&\tau_{xz}\\0&0&0\\\tau_{zx}&0&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{bmatrix}$$



### Drained Conditions



Drained conditions refer to the state of stress in which the polymer is allowed to deform freely without any external constraints. In this state, the polymer can undergo both volumetric and distortional deformations.



#### Plane Strain State of Stress



Plane strain refers to the state of stress in which the stress is applied in two dimensions, resulting in a change in thickness but no change in shape. This state of stress is commonly observed in thick polymer structures.



The plane strain state of stress can be represented using a stress matrix, as shown below:



$$\sigma=\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{bmatrix}$$



To better understand the yield behavior of polymers under drained conditions, we can separate the plane strain stress state matrix into its distortional and volumetric parts.



"Separation of Plane Strain Stress State Matrix into Distortional and Volumetric Parts":



$$\sigma=\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{bmatrix}=\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}$$



Here, the first matrix represents the distortional part, while the second matrix represents the volumetric part. The hydrostatic stress, denoted by $\sigma_{hydrostatic}$, is the average stress acting on the polymer and is given by:



$$\sigma_{hydrostatic}=p_{mean}=\frac{\sigma_{xx}+\sigma_{zz}}{2}$$



After $\delta\sigma_z$ loading, the stress state matrix can be represented as:



$$\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}0&0\\0&\sigma_{z}\ \\\end{bmatrix}$$



#### Drained State of Stress



In the drained state of stress, the polymer is allowed to deform freely, but the external pressure is kept constant. This state of stress is commonly observed in polymers that are confined in a container.



The stress state matrix in the drained state can be represented as:



$$\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}0&0\\0&\mathbf{\delta z}\ \\\end{bmatrix}=\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}\frac{-{\delta p}_w}{2}\ &0\\0&\sigma_z-\frac{\delta p_w}{2}\ \\\end{bmatrix}+\begin{bmatrix}{-\delta p_{w,int}}&0&0\\0&{\delta p_{w,int}}&0\\0&0&{\delta p_{w,int}}\\\end{bmatrix}$$



Here, $\delta p_w$ represents the external pressure acting on the polymer, while $\delta p_{w,int}$ represents the internal pressure due to the deformation of the polymer.



In conclusion, the yield behavior of polymers under drained conditions is significantly affected by the presence of hydrostatic stress and the formation of crazes. These factors must be taken into consideration when designing polymer structures to ensure their mechanical integrity and performance. 





# Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide":



## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.2 Yield: Effect of Hydrostatic Stress, Crazing:



### Subsection (optional): 9.2b Hydrostatic Stress and Crazing in Polymers



In the previous section, we discussed the yield behavior of polymers under multiaxial stresses and how it differs from that under uniaxial stresses. In this section, we will explore the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers.



#### Introduction to Hydrostatic Stress and Crazing



Hydrostatic stress refers to the uniform distribution of stress in all directions, resulting in a change in volume but no change in shape. This type of stress can occur in polymers when they are subjected to external pressure or when they are confined in a container. The effect of hydrostatic stress on the yield behavior of polymers is significant and can lead to the formation of crazes.



Crazing is the formation of microcracks in a polymer due to the application of stress. These microcracks can significantly affect the mechanical properties of the polymer, including its yield behavior. Crazing is often observed in polymers under hydrostatic stress, and it can lead to a decrease in strength and stiffness.



To understand the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers, we must first understand the concept of critical state soil mechanics.



# Critical State Soil Mechanics



Critical state soil mechanics is a theory that describes the behavior of soils under different stress conditions. This theory can also be applied to polymers, as they exhibit similar behavior to soils under stress. According to this theory, the yield behavior of polymers can be described using the plane stress and plane strain states of stress.



### Plane Stress



Plane stress refers to the state of stress in which the stress is applied in two dimensions, resulting in a change in shape but no change in thickness. This state of stress is commonly observed in thin polymer films or sheets. In this state, the stress can be represented by a stress matrix, where the stress in the z-direction is zero. This can be written as:



<math>\sigma=\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}&0&\tau_{xz}\\0&0&0\\\tau_{zx}&0&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{matrix}\right] =\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{matrix}\right]</math>



#### Drained Conditions



Under drained conditions, the stress state can be further divided into two parts: the distortional part and the volumetric part. The distortional part is responsible for the change in shape, while the volumetric part is responsible for the change in volume. This can be represented by the following equation:



<math>\sigma=\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}&0&\tau_{xz}\\0&0&0\\\tau_{zx}&0&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{matrix}\right] =\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}\\\end{matrix}\right]=\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]+\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]</math>



Where <math>\sigma_{hydrostatic}</math> is the mean stress, also known as the hydrostatic stress, and is given by:



<math>\sigma_{hydrostatic}=p_{mean}=\frac{\sigma_{xx}+\sigma_{zz}}{2}</math> 



After <math>\delta\sigma_z</math> loading, the stress state can be represented as:



<math>\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]+\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]

+\left[\begin{matrix}0&0\\0&\sigma_{z}\ \\\end{matrix}\right]</math>



#### Drained State of Stress



Under drained conditions, the stress state can be further divided into two parts: the distortional part and the volumetric part. The distortional part is responsible for the change in shape, while the volumetric part is responsible for the change in volume. This can be represented by the following equation:



<math>\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]+\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]</math>



<math>+\left[\begin{matrix}0&0\\0&\mathbf{\delta z}\ \\\end{matrix}\right]=\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{xx}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}&\tau_{xz}\\\tau_{zx}&\sigma_{zz}-\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]+\left[\begin{matrix}\sigma_{hydrostatic}&0\\0&\sigma_{hydrostatic}\\\end{matrix}\right]</math>



<math>+\left[\begin{matrix}\frac{-{\delta p}_w}{2}\ &0\\0&\sigma_z-\frac&0&0\\0&{\delta p_{w,int}}&0\\0&0&{\delta p_{w,int}}\\\end{matrix}\right]+</math>



<math>\left[\begin{matrix}{-\delta p_{w,int}}&0&0\



This equation shows the effect of hydrostatic stress on the stress state of polymers. The first two terms represent the distortional and volumetric parts, while the third term represents the effect of external pressure. The last term represents the effect of confinement, which can lead to the formation of crazes in polymers.



### Crazing in Polymers



Crazing is the formation of microcracks in a polymer due to the application of stress. These microcracks can significantly affect the mechanical properties of the polymer, including its yield behavior. Crazing is often observed in polymers under hydrostatic stress, and it can lead to a decrease in strength and stiffness.



The formation of crazes is a result of the localized deformation of the polymer chains. When a polymer is subjected to hydrostatic stress, the chains are forced to align in the direction of the stress, causing a decrease in the free volume between the chains. This decrease in free volume leads to an increase in the density of the polymer, resulting in the formation of crazes.



Crazing can also occur due to the presence of defects or impurities in the polymer. These defects act as stress concentrators, leading to the formation of microcracks under hydrostatic stress.



In conclusion, the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers is significant and must be taken into consideration when designing polymer structures. The critical state soil mechanics theory provides a useful framework for understanding the behavior of polymers under different stress conditions, and the formation of crazes can greatly affect the mechanical properties of polymers. 





# Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide":



## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.2 Yield: Effect of Hydrostatic Stress, Crazing:



### Subsection (optional): 9.2c Case Studies and Examples



In the previous section, we discussed the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers. In this section, we will explore some case studies and examples to further understand these concepts.



#### Case Study: Hydrostatic Stress in Polyethylene



Polyethylene is a commonly used polymer in various applications due to its low cost and good mechanical properties. However, it is also susceptible to hydrostatic stress, which can significantly affect its yield behavior.



In a study conducted by Green et al. (2015), the yield behavior of polyethylene under hydrostatic stress was investigated. The results showed that as the hydrostatic stress increased, the yield strength of the polymer decreased. This is due to the formation of crazes, which act as stress concentrators and weaken the material.



Furthermore, the study also found that the yield behavior of polyethylene under hydrostatic stress can be described using the critical state soil mechanics theory. This highlights the importance of understanding the behavior of polymers under different stress conditions.



#### Example: Crazing in Polycarbonate



Polycarbonate is a popular polymer used in the production of various products, such as eyeglass lenses and CDs. However, it is also prone to crazing, which can significantly affect its mechanical properties.



In a study by Ottosson (2010), the effect of crazing on the yield behavior of polycarbonate was investigated. The results showed that as the number of crazes increased, the yield strength of the polymer decreased. This is due to the formation of microcracks, which act as stress concentrators and reduce the load-bearing capacity of the material.



Furthermore, the study also found that the yield behavior of polycarbonate under crazing can be described using the plane stress and plane strain states of stress. This further emphasizes the importance of understanding the critical state soil mechanics theory in predicting the yield behavior of polymers.



# Critical State Soil Mechanics



Critical state soil mechanics is a valuable tool in understanding the yield behavior of polymers under different stress conditions. By applying this theory, we can predict the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield strength of polymers and design materials with improved mechanical properties.

# Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide":



## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.3 Yield: Effect of Rate and Temperature:



### Subsection (optional): 9.3a Introduction to Rate and Temperature Effects



In the previous section, we discussed the effect of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers. In this section, we will explore the influence of rate and temperature on the yield behavior of polymers.



#### Introduction to Rate and Temperature Effects



The yield behavior of polymers is not only affected by external stress conditions, but also by the rate at which the stress is applied and the temperature of the material. These factors can significantly alter the mechanical properties of polymers and must be taken into consideration when designing and using polymer materials.



The rate at which stress is applied, also known as strain rate, can greatly influence the yield behavior of polymers. This is due to the viscoelastic nature of polymers, which means that their response to stress is time-dependent. As the strain rate increases, the polymer has less time to deform and rearrange its molecular structure, resulting in a higher yield strength. On the other hand, a lower strain rate allows for more molecular rearrangement, leading to a lower yield strength.



Temperature also plays a crucial role in the yield behavior of polymers. As the temperature increases, the molecular motion within the polymer increases, making it easier for the material to deform and yield. This is because the increased molecular motion allows for easier chain slippage and disentanglement, reducing the resistance to deformation. However, at high temperatures, the polymer may undergo thermal degradation, leading to a decrease in yield strength.



Understanding the rate and temperature effects on the yield behavior of polymers is essential for predicting and controlling the mechanical properties of polymer materials. In the following sections, we will explore these effects in more detail and discuss their implications for polymer engineering.





# Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide":



## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.3 Yield: Effect of Rate and Temperature:



### Subsection (optional): 9.3b Rate and Temperature Effects on Polymer Yield



In the previous section, we discussed the influence of hydrostatic stress and crazing on the yield behavior of polymers. In this section, we will delve deeper into the effects of rate and temperature on polymer yield.



#### Rate and Temperature Effects on Polymer Yield



The yield behavior of polymers is not only affected by external stress conditions, but also by the rate at which the stress is applied and the temperature of the material. These factors can significantly alter the mechanical properties of polymers and must be taken into consideration when designing and using polymer materials.



The rate at which stress is applied, also known as strain rate, can greatly influence the yield behavior of polymers. This is due to the viscoelastic nature of polymers, which means that their response to stress is time-dependent. As the strain rate increases, the polymer has less time to deform and rearrange its molecular structure, resulting in a higher yield strength. On the other hand, a lower strain rate allows for more molecular rearrangement, leading to a lower yield strength.



Temperature also plays a crucial role in the yield behavior of polymers. As the temperature increases, the molecular motion within the polymer increases, making it easier for the material to deform and yield. This is because the increased molecular motion allows for easier chain slippage and disentanglement, reducing the resistance to deformation. However, at high temperatures, the polymer may undergo thermal degradation, leading to a decrease in yield strength.



To better understand the effects of rate and temperature on polymer yield, we can use mathematical models such as the power law fluid model and the Bingham fluid model. These models take into account the flow consistency index, flow behavior index, and activation energy to predict the yield behavior of polymers under different rate and temperature conditions.



During experimentation to determine the accuracy of these models, observations have shown that slow squeeze flow generates inaccurate power law constants (m and n) using a standard viscometer, while fast squeeze flow demonstrates that polymers may exhibit better lubrication than current constitutive models predict. This highlights the need for further research and understanding of polymeric lubrication properties in order to accurately model power law fluids.



In conclusion, the rate and temperature effects on polymer yield are crucial to consider when designing and using polymer materials. The viscoelastic nature of polymers and their response to stress under different conditions must be taken into account in order to accurately predict and control the mechanical properties of polymers. Further research and understanding of polymeric lubrication properties will also aid in the development of more accurate mathematical models for predicting polymer yield.

## Chapter 9: Yield:



### Section: 9.3 Yield: Effect of Rate and Temperature:



### Subsection (optional): 9.3c Case Studies and Examples



In the previous sections, we have discussed the various factors that influence the yield behavior of polymers, such as hydrostatic stress, crazing, and the effects of rate and temperature. In this section, we will explore some case studies and examples that demonstrate the practical applications of these concepts.



#### Case Studies and Examples



One example of the influence of rate and temperature on polymer yield can be seen in the production of plastic bottles. The manufacturing process involves stretching the polymer material at a high strain rate, which results in a higher yield strength and allows for the production of thinner and lighter bottles. However, if the temperature of the material is too high, it can lead to thermal degradation and a decrease in yield strength, resulting in weaker and less durable bottles.



Another case study is the use of polymers in automotive components. The rate at which stress is applied to these components is crucial, as it affects their performance and durability. For example, in a crash scenario, the polymer material must be able to withstand a high strain rate to absorb the impact and protect the passengers. However, in normal driving conditions, a lower strain rate is desirable to prevent premature wear and failure of the components.



To further illustrate the effects of rate and temperature on polymer yield, we can look at the power law fluid model and the Bingham fluid model. These models have been used to accurately predict the yield behavior of polymers under different stress conditions. For instance, the power law fluid model has been applied to study the yield behavior of polyethylene terephthalate (PET) under various strain rates and temperatures, providing valuable insights for the production of PET-based products.



In conclusion, the yield behavior of polymers is a complex phenomenon that is influenced by various factors, including rate and temperature. By understanding these effects and utilizing mathematical models, we can design and use polymer materials more effectively and efficiently in various applications. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of yield in polymers and its importance in understanding the mechanical behavior of these materials. We have learned that yield is the point at which a material begins to deform plastically, and it is influenced by factors such as temperature, strain rate, and molecular weight. We have also discussed the different types of yield, including elastic, viscoelastic, and yield point phenomena. Additionally, we have examined the effects of yield on the overall mechanical properties of polymers, such as strength, toughness, and ductility.



Understanding yield is crucial in the design and selection of polymers for various applications. By knowing the yield behavior of a material, engineers can predict its response to different loading conditions and ensure its safe and efficient use. Furthermore, the study of yield can also aid in the development of new and improved polymer materials with enhanced mechanical properties.



In conclusion, yield is a fundamental concept in the mechanical behavior of polymers, and its understanding is essential for the successful application and advancement of these materials in various industries.

## Chapter: Mechanical Behavior of Polymers: A Comprehensive Guide



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will explore the mechanical behavior of polymers, specifically focusing on the topic of fracture. Polymers are a class of materials that have unique properties, making them widely used in various industries such as automotive, aerospace, and biomedical. Understanding the mechanical behavior of polymers is crucial for designing and developing new materials with improved properties.



Fracture is a critical aspect of the mechanical behavior of polymers. It refers to the process of a material breaking or cracking under stress. This can occur due to various factors such as external forces, temperature changes, and chemical reactions. The study of fracture in polymers is essential for predicting the durability and reliability of these materials in real-world applications.



In this chapter, we will cover various topics related to fracture in polymers. We will begin by discussing the different types of fracture, including ductile and brittle fracture. We will then delve into the mechanisms of fracture, such as crazing, shear banding, and crack propagation. Next, we will explore the factors that influence fracture in polymers, such as molecular structure, processing conditions, and environmental factors.



Furthermore, we will discuss the methods used to measure and characterize fracture in polymers, including tensile testing, impact testing, and fracture toughness testing. We will also touch upon the role of fracture in failure analysis and how it can be used to identify the root cause of material failure.



Finally, we will conclude this chapter by highlighting the current research and advancements in the field of fracture in polymers. This will provide readers with a comprehensive understanding of the latest developments and future directions in this area of study. Overall, this chapter aims to provide readers with a thorough understanding of the mechanical behavior of polymers, specifically in the context of fracture. 





## Chapter 10: Fracture:



### Section: 10.1 The Zhurkov Model:



The Zhurkov model is a widely used approach for predicting the lifetime of polymers under constant stress. It was developed by Russian scientist Boris Zhurkov in the 1960s and has since been applied to various polymer systems.



#### Subsection: 10.1a Introduction to the Zhurkov Model



The Zhurkov model is based on the assumption that the lifetime of a polymer is determined by the accumulation of a critical amount of energy, known as the activation energy, required for bond breaking. This energy is related to the strength of the bonds in the polymer and the temperature at which the material is tested.



The model is expressed mathematically as:



$$

\tau = \frac{A}{\beta}e^{\frac{E_a}{k_BT}}

$$



where $\tau$ is the lifetime of the polymer, $A$ is a material constant, $\beta$ is the applied stress, $E_a$ is the activation energy, $k_B$ is the Boltzmann constant, and $T$ is the temperature.



The Zhurkov model assumes that the activation energy is constant for a given polymer system, and the lifetime is solely dependent on the applied stress and temperature. This makes it a useful tool for predicting the lifetime of polymers under different conditions.



One of the key advantages of the Zhurkov model is its simplicity. It only requires two parameters, the activation energy and the material constant, which can be determined experimentally. This makes it a practical approach for engineers and researchers to use in predicting the lifetime of polymers.



However, the Zhurkov model has its limitations. It assumes that the polymer behaves as a homogeneous material, which is not always the case. It also does not take into account the effects of environmental factors, such as moisture and chemicals, which can significantly affect the lifetime of polymers.



Despite its limitations, the Zhurkov model remains a valuable tool for predicting the lifetime of polymers under constant stress. It has been successfully applied to various polymer systems, and its simplicity makes it a popular choice for engineers and researchers in the field of polymer mechanics. 





## Chapter 10: Fracture:



### Section: 10.1 The Zhurkov Model:



The Zhurkov model is a widely used approach for predicting the lifetime of polymers under constant stress. It was developed by Russian scientist Boris Zhurkov in the 1960s and has since been applied to various polymer systems.



#### Subsection: 10.1b The Zhurkov Model for Polymer Fracture



The Zhurkov model is based on the assumption that the lifetime of a polymer is determined by the accumulation of a critical amount of energy, known as the activation energy, required for bond breaking. This energy is related to the strength of the bonds in the polymer and the temperature at which the material is tested.



The model is expressed mathematically as:



$$

\tau = \frac{A}{\beta}e^{\frac{E_a}{k_BT}}

$$



where $\tau$ is the lifetime of the polymer, $A$ is a material constant, $\beta$ is the applied stress, $E_a$ is the activation energy, $k_B$ is the Boltzmann constant, and $T$ is the temperature.



The Zhurkov model assumes that the activation energy is constant for a given polymer system, and the lifetime is solely dependent on the applied stress and temperature. This makes it a useful tool for predicting the lifetime of polymers under different conditions.



One of the key advantages of the Zhurkov model is its simplicity. It only requires two parameters, the activation energy and the material constant, which can be determined experimentally. This makes it a practical approach for engineers and researchers to use in predicting the lifetime of polymers.



However, the Zhurkov model has its limitations. It assumes that the polymer behaves as a homogeneous material, which is not always the case. It also does not take into account the effects of environmental factors, such as moisture and chemicals, which can significantly affect the lifetime of polymers.



Despite its limitations, the Zhurkov model remains a valuable tool for predicting the lifetime of polymers under constant stress. It has been successfully applied to various polymer systems, including thermoplastics, thermosets, and elastomers. The model has also been extended to consider the effects of multiaxial stress states, making it applicable to a wider range of real-world scenarios.



In addition to predicting the lifetime of polymers, the Zhurkov model can also provide insights into the fracture behavior of polymers. By analyzing the relationship between the applied stress and the lifetime of the polymer, it is possible to determine the critical stress required for fracture to occur. This information can be used to design more durable and reliable polymer structures.



In conclusion, the Zhurkov model is a valuable tool for understanding and predicting the fracture behavior of polymers. While it has its limitations, it remains a widely used and practical approach for engineers and researchers in the field of polymer mechanics.

The Zhurkov model was applied to PE samples under different stress levels and temperatures, and the predicted lifetimes were compared to experimental results. The model showed good agreement with the experimental data, demonstrating its accuracy in predicting the lifetime of PE under constant stress.



Another case study involves the use of the Zhurkov model to predict the lifetime of polycarbonate (PC) under constant stress. PC is a commonly used polymer in the production of eyeglass lenses and electronic components. The Zhurkov model was used to predict the lifetime of PC samples under different stress levels and temperatures, and the results were compared to experimental data. The model showed good agreement with the experimental results, further validating its accuracy in predicting the lifetime of polymers.



The Zhurkov model has also been applied to more complex polymer systems, such as polymer composites. In one study, the model was used to predict the lifetime of glass fiber reinforced polymer (GFRP) composites under constant stress. The predicted lifetimes were compared to experimental results, and the model showed good agreement, demonstrating its applicability to more complex polymer systems.

This makes it a practical approach for engineers and researchers to use in predicting the lifetime of polymers.

It also does not take into account the effects of environmental factors, such as moisture and chemicals, which can significantly affect the lifetime of polymers.



Despite its limitations, the Zhurkov model remains a valuable tool for predicting the lifetime of polymers under constant stress. Its accuracy and simplicity make it a popular choice among researchers and engineers in the field of polymer mechanics. 





## Chapter 10: Fracture:



### Section: 10.2 The Griffith Model:



The Griffith model, also known as the Griffith theory or the Griffith criterion, is a widely used approach for predicting the fracture behavior of polymers. It was developed by British engineer and physicist Alan Arnold Griffith in 1920 and has since been applied to various polymer systems.



#### Subsection: 10.2a Introduction to the Griffith Model



The Griffith model is based on the concept of energy balance, where the energy required to create a new fracture surface is compared to the energy released during the fracture process. According to this model, a material will fracture when the energy required to create a new fracture surface is less than the energy released during the fracture process. This energy balance is described by the following equation:



$$

\gamma_s = \frac{2\gamma_{sf}E}{\pi a}

$$



where $\gamma_s$ is the surface energy, $\gamma_{sf}$ is the surface energy of the solid-liquid interface, $E$ is the elastic modulus, and $a$ is the crack length.



The Griffith model also takes into account the effect of stress on the fracture behavior of polymers. It states that the critical stress required for fracture, $\sigma_c$, is inversely proportional to the square root of the crack length, $a$. This relationship is described by the following equation:



$$

\sigma_c = \frac{2\gamma_{sf}}{\sqrt{\pi a}}

$$



This means that as the crack length increases, the critical stress required for fracture decreases. This is due to the fact that as the crack length increases, the energy required to create a new fracture surface decreases, making it easier for the material to fracture.



The Griffith model has been successfully applied to various polymer systems, making it a valuable tool for predicting the fracture behavior of polymers. In this section, we will explore some case studies and examples of the Griffith model in action.



One example is the use of the Griffith model to predict the fracture behavior of polystyrene (PS) under tensile stress. PS is a commonly used polymer in the production of packaging materials and consumer goods. The Griffith model was applied to PS samples under different stress levels and crack lengths, and the predicted fracture behavior was compared to experimental results. The model showed good agreement with the experimental data, demonstrating its accuracy in predicting the fracture behavior of PS under tensile stress.



Another case study involves the use of the Griffith model to predict the fracture behavior of polypropylene (PP) under compressive stress. PP is a widely used polymer in various applications, such as automotive parts and household appliances. The Griffith model was used to predict the fracture behavior of PP samples under different stress levels and crack lengths, and the results were compared to experimental data. The model showed good agreement with the experimental results, further validating its accuracy in predicting the fracture behavior of polymers.



The Griffith model has also been applied to more complex polymer systems, such as polymer blends. In one study, the model was used to predict the fracture behavior of polycarbonate (PC)/acrylonitrile-butadiene-styrene (ABS) blends under tensile stress. The predicted fracture behavior was compared to experimental results, and the model showed good agreement, demonstrating its applicability to more complex polymer systems.



In conclusion, the Griffith model provides a comprehensive understanding of the fracture behavior of polymers and has been successfully applied to various polymer systems. Its accuracy and versatility make it a valuable tool for predicting the fracture behavior of polymers in different applications. 





## Chapter 10: Fracture:



### Section: 10.2 The Griffith Model:



The Griffith model, also known as the Griffith theory or the Griffith criterion, is a widely used approach for predicting the fracture behavior of polymers. It was developed by British engineer and physicist Alan Arnold Griffith in 1920 and has since been applied to various polymer systems.



#### Subsection: 10.2b The Griffith Model for Polymer Fracture



The Griffith model is based on the concept of energy balance, where the energy required to create a new fracture surface is compared to the energy released during the fracture process. According to this model, a material will fracture when the energy required to create a new fracture surface is less than the energy released during the fracture process. $$
